<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1236090951">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about how a computer organizes and accesses computer files. </tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1129693374" /><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1239400231">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}}@media print{.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:none!important}}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:OS" title="Template:OS"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:OS" title="Template talk:OS"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:OS" title="Special:EditPage/Template:OS"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Computing" title="Computing">computing</a>, a <b>file system</b> or <b>filesystem</b> (often abbreviated to <b>FS</b> or <b>fs</b>) governs <a href="/wiki/Computer_file" title="Computer file">file</a> organization and access. A <i>local</i> file system is a capability of an <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating system</a> that services the applications running on the same <a href="/wiki/Computer" title="Computer">computer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Distributed_file_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Distributed file system">distributed file system</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Communication_protocol" title="Communication protocol">protocol</a> that provides file access between <a href="/wiki/Computer_network" title="Computer network">networked</a> computers.
</p><p>A file system provides a <a href="/wiki/Computer_data_storage" title="Computer data storage">data storage</a> <a href="/wiki/Service_(systems_architecture)" title="Service (systems architecture)">service</a> that allows <a href="/wiki/Application_software" title="Application software">applications</a> to share <a href="/wiki/Mass_storage" title="Mass storage">mass storage</a>. Without a file system, applications could access the storage in <a href="/wiki/Software_incompatibility" title="Software incompatibility">incompatible</a> ways that lead to <a href="/wiki/Resource_contention" title="Resource contention">resource contention</a>, <a href="/wiki/Data_corruption" title="Data corruption">data corruption</a> and <a href="/wiki/Data_loss" title="Data loss">data loss</a>.
</p><p>There are many file system <a href="/wiki/Software_design" title="Software design">designs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Implementation" title="Implementation">implementations</a> – with various structure and features and various resulting characteristics such as speed, flexibility, security, size and more.
</p><p>Files systems have been developed for many types of <a href="/wiki/Computer_storage_device" class="mw-redirect" title="Computer storage device">storage devices</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Hard_disk_drive" title="Hard disk drive">hard disk drives</a> (HDDs), <a href="/wiki/Solid-state_drive" title="Solid-state drive">solid-state drives</a> (SSDs), <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_tape" title="Magnetic tape">magnetic tapes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Optical_disc" title="Optical disc">optical discs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A portion of the computer <a href="/wiki/Random-access_memory" title="Random-access memory">main memory</a> can be set up as a <a href="/wiki/RAM_disk" class="mw-redirect" title="RAM disk">RAM disk</a> that serves as a storage device for a file system. File systems such as <a href="/wiki/Tmpfs" title="Tmpfs">tmpfs</a> can store files in <a href="/wiki/Virtual_memory" title="Virtual memory">virtual memory</a>.
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="VIRTUAL-FILE"></span>
A <i>virtual</i> file system provides access to files that are either computed on request, called <i>virtual files</i> (see <a href="/wiki/Procfs" title="Procfs">procfs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sysfs" title="Sysfs">sysfs</a>), or are mapping into another, backing storage.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Etymology">Etymology</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>From <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1900</span> and before the advent of computers the terms <i>file system</i>, <i>filing system</i> and <i>system for filing</i> were used to describe methods of organizing, storing and retrieving paper documents.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By 1961, the term <i>file system</i> was being applied to computerized filing alongside the original meaning.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By 1964, it was in general use.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Architecture">Architecture</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Architecture"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A local file system's <a href="/wiki/Software_architecture" title="Software architecture">architecture</a> can be described as <a href="/wiki/Abstraction_layer" title="Abstraction layer">layers of abstraction</a> even though a particular file system design may not actually separate the concepts.<sup id="cite_ref-JHU_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JHU-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>logical file system</i> layer provides relatively high-level access via an <a href="/wiki/Application_programming_interface" class="mw-redirect" title="Application programming interface">application programming interface</a> (API) for file operations including open, close, read and write – delegating operations to lower layers. This layer manages open file table entries and per-process file descriptors.<sup id="cite_ref-IBMKC_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IBMKC-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It provides file access, directory operations, security and protection.<sup id="cite_ref-JHU_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JHU-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>virtual file system</i>, an optional layer, supports multiple concurrent instances of physical file systems, each of which called a file system implementation.<sup id="cite_ref-IBMKC_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IBMKC-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>physical file system</i> layer provides relatively low-level access to a storage device (e.g. disk). It reads and writes <a href="/wiki/Block_(data_storage)" title="Block (data storage)">data blocks</a>, provides <a href="/wiki/Data_buffer" title="Data buffer">buffering</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Memory_management" title="Memory management">memory management</a> and controls placement of blocks in specific locations on the storage medium. This layer uses <a href="/wiki/Device_driver" title="Device driver">device drivers</a> or <a href="/wiki/Channel_I/O" title="Channel I/O">channel I/O</a> to drive the storage device.<sup id="cite_ref-JHU_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JHU-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Attributes">Attributes</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Attributes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="File_names">File names</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: File names"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Filename" title="Filename">Filename</a></div>
<p>A <b>file name</b>, or <b>filename</b>, identifies a file to consuming applications and in some cases users.
</p><p>A file name is unique so that an application can refer to exactly one file for a particular name. If the file system supports directories, then generally file name uniqueness is enforced within the context of each directory. In other words, a storage can contain multiple files with the same name, but not in the same directory.
</p><p>Most file systems restrict the length of a file name.
</p><p>Some file systems match file names as <a href="/wiki/Case_sensitivity" title="Case sensitivity">case sensitive</a> and others as case insensitive. For example, the names <code>MYFILE</code> and <code>myfile</code> match the same file for case insensitive, but different files for case sensitive.
</p><p>Most modern file systems allow a file name to contain a wide range of characters from the <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> character set. Some restrict characters such as those used to indicate special attributes such as a device, device type, directory prefix, file path separator, or file type.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Directories">Directories</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Directories"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Directory_(computing)" title="Directory (computing)">Directory (computing)</a></div>
<p>File systems typically support organizing files into <b>directories</b>, also called <b>folders</b>, which segregate files into groups.
</p><p>This may be implemented by associating the file name with an index in a <a href="/wiki/Table_of_contents" title="Table of contents">table of contents</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Inode" title="Inode">inode</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Unix-like" title="Unix-like">Unix-like</a> file system.
</p><p>Directory structures may be flat (i.e. linear), or allow hierarchies by allowing a directory to contain directories, called subdirectories.
</p><p>The first file system to support arbitrary hierarchies of directories was used in the <a href="/wiki/Multics" title="Multics">Multics</a> operating system.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The native file systems of Unix-like systems also support arbitrary directory hierarchies, as do, <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Hierarchical_File_System_(Apple)" title="Hierarchical File System (Apple)">Hierarchical File System</a> and its successor <a href="/wiki/HFS_Plus" title="HFS Plus">HFS+</a> in <a href="/wiki/Classic_Mac_OS" title="Classic Mac OS">classic Mac OS</a>, the <a href="/wiki/File_Allocation_Table" title="File Allocation Table">FAT</a> file system in <a href="/wiki/MS-DOS" title="MS-DOS">MS-DOS</a> 2.0 and later versions of MS-DOS and in <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a>, the <a href="/wiki/NTFS" title="NTFS">NTFS</a> file system in the <a href="/wiki/Windows_NT" title="Windows NT">Windows NT</a> family of operating systems, and the ODS-2 (On-Disk Structure-2) and higher levels of the <a href="/wiki/Files-11" title="Files-11">Files-11</a> file system in <a href="/wiki/OpenVMS" title="OpenVMS">OpenVMS</a>.
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="METADATA"></span>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Metadata">Metadata</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Metadata"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In addition to data, the file content, a file system also manages associated <a href="/wiki/Metadata" title="Metadata">metadata</a> which may include but is not limited to:
</p>
<ul><li>name</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/File_size" title="File size">size</a> which may be stored as the number of blocks allocated or as a <a href="/wiki/Byte" title="Byte">byte</a> count</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/System_time" title="System time">when</a> created, last accessed, last backed-up</li>
<li>owner <a href="/wiki/User_ID" class="mw-redirect" title="User ID">user</a> and <a href="/wiki/Group_ID" class="mw-redirect" title="Group ID">group</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/File_system_permissions" class="mw-redirect" title="File system permissions">access permissions</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/File_attribute" title="File attribute">file attributes</a> such as whether the file is read-only, <a href="/wiki/Executable" title="Executable">executable</a>, etc.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Device_file" title="Device file">device type</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Block_devices" class="mw-redirect" title="Block devices">block</a>, <a href="/wiki/Character_devices" class="mw-redirect" title="Character devices">character</a>, <a href="/wiki/Internet_socket" class="mw-redirect" title="Internet socket">socket</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subdirectory" class="mw-redirect" title="Subdirectory">subdirectory</a>, etc.)</li></ul>
<p>A file system stores associated metadata separate from the content of the file.
</p><p>Most file systems store the names of all the files in one directory in one place—the directory table for that directory—which is often stored like any other file.
Many file systems put only some of the metadata for a file in the directory table, and the rest of the metadata for that file in a completely separate structure, such as the <a href="/wiki/Inode" title="Inode">inode</a>.
</p><p>Most file systems also store metadata not associated with any one particular file.
Such metadata includes information about unused regions—<a href="/wiki/Free_space_bitmap" class="mw-redirect" title="Free space bitmap">free space bitmap</a>, <a href="/wiki/Block_availability_map" title="Block availability map">block availability map</a>—and information about <a href="/wiki/Bad_sector" title="Bad sector">bad sectors</a>.
Often such information about an <a href="/wiki/Allocation_group" title="Allocation group">allocation group</a> is stored inside the allocation group itself.
</p><p>Additional attributes can be associated on file systems, such as <a href="/wiki/NTFS" title="NTFS">NTFS</a>, <a href="/wiki/XFS" title="XFS">XFS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ext2" title="Ext2">ext2</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ext3" title="Ext3">ext3</a>, some versions of <a href="/wiki/Unix_File_System" title="Unix File System">UFS</a>, and <a href="/wiki/HFS%2B" class="mw-redirect" title="HFS+">HFS+</a>, using <a href="/wiki/Extended_file_attributes" title="Extended file attributes">extended file attributes</a>. Some file systems provide for user defined attributes such as the author of the document, the character encoding of a document or the size of an image.
</p><p>Some file systems allow for different data collections to be associated with one file name. These separate collections may be referred to as <i>streams</i> or <i>forks</i>. Apple has long used a forked file system on the Macintosh, and Microsoft supports streams in NTFS. Some file systems maintain multiple past revisions of a file under a single file name; the file name by itself retrieves the most recent version, while prior saved version can be accessed using a special naming convention such as "filename;4" or "filename(-4)" to access the version four saves ago.
</p><p>See <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_file_systems#Metadata" title="Comparison of file systems">comparison of file systems § Metadata</a> for details on which file systems support which kinds of metadata.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Storage_space_organization">Storage space organization</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Storage space organization"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A local file system tracks which areas of storage belong to which file and which are not being used.
</p><p>When a file system creates a file, it allocates space for data. Some file systems permit or require specifying an initial space allocation and subsequent incremental allocations as the file grows.
</p><p>To delete a file, the file system records that the file's space is free; available to use for another file.
</p>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Frame"><a href="/wiki/File:100_000-files_5-bytes_each_--_400_megs_of_slack_space.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9a/100_000-files_5-bytes_each_--_400_megs_of_slack_space.png" decoding="async" width="372" height="135" class="mw-file-element" data-file-width="372" data-file-height="135" /></a><figcaption>An example of slack space, demonstrated with 4,096-<a href="/wiki/Byte" title="Byte">byte</a> NTFS clusters: 100,000 files, each five bytes per file, which equal to 500,000 bytes of actual data but require 409,600,000 bytes of disk space to store </figcaption></figure>
<p>A local file system manages storage space to provide a level of reliability and efficiency. Generally, it allocates storage device space in a granular manner, usually multiple physical units (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Bytes" class="mw-redirect" title="Bytes">bytes</a>). For example, in <a href="/wiki/Apple_DOS" title="Apple DOS">Apple DOS</a> of the early 1980s, 256-byte sectors on 140 kilobyte floppy disk used a <i>track/sector map</i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The granular nature results in unused space, sometimes called <a href="/wiki/Slack_space" class="mw-redirect" title="Slack space">slack space</a>, for each file except for those that have the rare size that is a multiple of the granular allocation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarrier2005187–188_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarrier2005187–188-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For a 512-byte allocation, the average unused space is 256 bytes. For 64&#160;KB clusters, the average unused space is 32&#160;KB.
</p><p>Generally, the allocation unit size is set when the storage is configured.
Choosing a relatively small size compared to the files stored, results in excessive access overhead.
Choosing a relatively large size results in excessive unused space.
Choosing an allocation size based on the average size of files expected to be in the storage tends to minimize unusable space.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Fragmentation">Fragmentation</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Fragmentation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:File_system_fragmentation.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/File_system_fragmentation.svg/220px-File_system_fragmentation.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="93" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/File_system_fragmentation.svg/330px-File_system_fragmentation.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/File_system_fragmentation.svg/440px-File_system_fragmentation.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="488" data-file-height="206" /></a><figcaption>File systems may become <a href="/wiki/File_system_fragmentation" title="File system fragmentation">fragmented</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>As a file system creates, modifies and deletes files, the underlying storage representation may become <a href="/wiki/File_system_fragmentation" title="File system fragmentation">fragmented</a>. Files and the unused space between files will occupy allocation blocks that are not contiguous.
</p><p>A file becomes fragmented if space needed to store its content cannot be allocated in contiguous blocks. Free space becomes fragmented when files are deleted.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>This is invisible to the end user and the system still works correctly. However this can degrade performance on some storage hardware that work better with contiguous blocks such as <a href="/wiki/Hard_disk_drive#Performance_characteristics" title="Hard disk drive">hard disk drives</a>. Other hardware such as <a href="/wiki/Solid-state_drive" title="Solid-state drive">solid-state drives</a> are not affected by fragmentation.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Access_control">Access control</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Access control"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Computer_security" title="Computer security">Computer security</a>, <a href="/wiki/Password_cracking" title="Password cracking">Password cracking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Filesystem-level_encryption" title="Filesystem-level encryption">Filesystem-level encryption</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Encrypting_File_System" title="Encrypting File System">Encrypting File System</a></div>
<p>A file system often supports access control of data that it manages.
</p><p>The intent of access control is often to prevent certain users from reading or modifying certain files.
</p><p>Access control can also restrict access by program in order to ensure that data is modified in a controlled way. Examples include passwords stored in the metadata of the file or elsewhere and <a href="/wiki/File_permissions" class="mw-redirect" title="File permissions">file permissions</a> in the form of permission bits, <a href="/wiki/Access_control_list" class="mw-redirect" title="Access control list">access control lists</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Capability-based_security" title="Capability-based security">capabilities</a>. The need for file system utilities to be able to access the data at the media level to reorganize the structures and provide efficient backup usually means that these are only effective for polite users but are not effective against intruders.
</p><p>Methods for encrypting file data are sometimes included in the file system. This is very effective since there is no need for file system utilities to know the encryption seed to effectively manage the data. The risks of relying on encryption include the fact that an attacker can copy the data and use brute force to decrypt the data. Additionally, losing the seed means losing the data.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Storage_quota">Storage quota</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Storage quota"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Btrfs_qgroup_screenshot.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Btrfs_qgroup_screenshot.png/330px-Btrfs_qgroup_screenshot.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="324" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Btrfs_qgroup_screenshot.png/500px-Btrfs_qgroup_screenshot.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/11/Btrfs_qgroup_screenshot.png 2x" data-file-width="517" data-file-height="508" /></a><figcaption>Example of qgroup (quota group) of a <a href="/wiki/Btrfs" title="Btrfs">btrfs</a> filesystem</figcaption></figure>
<p>Some operating systems allow a system administrator to enable <a href="/wiki/Disk_quota" title="Disk quota">disk quotas</a> to limit a user's use of storage space.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Data_integrity">Data integrity</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Data integrity"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A file system typically ensures that stored data remains consistent in both normal operations as well as exceptional situations like:
</p>
<ul><li>accessing program neglects to inform the file system that it has completed file access (to close a file)</li>
<li>accessing program terminates abnormally (crashes)</li>
<li>media failure</li>
<li>loss of connection to remote systems</li>
<li>operating system failure</li>
<li>system reset (<a href="/wiki/Warm_reboot" class="mw-redirect" title="Warm reboot">soft reboot</a>)</li>
<li>power failure (<a href="/wiki/Hard_reboot" class="mw-redirect" title="Hard reboot">hard reboot</a>)</li></ul>
<p>Recovery from exceptional situations may include updating metadata, directory entries and handling data that was buffered but not written to storage media.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Recording">Recording</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Recording"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A file system might record events to allow analysis of issues such as:
</p>
<ul><li>file or systemic problems and performance</li>
<li>nefarious access</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Data_access">Data access</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Data access"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Byte_stream_access">Byte stream access</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Byte stream access"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Many file systems access data as a stream of <a href="/wiki/Bytes" class="mw-redirect" title="Bytes">bytes</a>. Typically, to read file data, a program provides a <a href="/wiki/Memory_buffer" class="mw-redirect" title="Memory buffer">memory buffer</a> and the file system retrieves data from the medium and then writes the data to the buffer. A write involves the program providing a buffer of bytes that the file system reads and then stores to the medium.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Record_access">Record access</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Record access"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Some file systems, or layers on top of a file system, allow a program to define a <a href="/wiki/Record_(computer_science)" title="Record (computer science)">record</a> so that a program can read and write data as a structure; not an unorganized sequence of bytes.
</p><p>If a <i>fixed length</i> record definition is used, then locating the n<sup>th</sup> record can be calculated mathematically, which is relatively fast compared to parsing the data for record separators.
</p><p>An identification for each record, also known as a key, allows a program to read, write and update records without regard to their location in storage. Such storage requires managing blocks of media, usually separating key blocks and data blocks. Efficient algorithms can be developed with pyramid structures for locating records.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Utilities">Utilities</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Utilities"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Typically, a file system can be managed by the user via various utility programs.
</p><p>Some utilities allow the user to create, configure and remove an instance of a file system. It may allow extending or truncating the space allocated to the file system.
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="DENTRY"></span>
Directory utilities may be used to create, rename and delete <i>directory entries</i>, which are also known as <i>dentries</i> (singular: <i>dentry</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and to alter metadata associated with a directory. Directory utilities may also include capabilities to create additional links to a directory (<a href="/wiki/Hard_link" title="Hard link">hard links</a> in <a href="/wiki/Unix" title="Unix">Unix</a>), to rename parent links (".." in <a href="/wiki/Unix-like" title="Unix-like">Unix-like</a> operating systems),<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (July 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and to create bidirectional links to files.
</p><p>File utilities create, list, copy, move and delete files, and alter metadata. They may be able to truncate data, truncate or extend space allocation, append to, move, and modify files in-place. Depending on the underlying structure of the file system, they may provide a mechanism to prepend to or truncate from the beginning of a file, insert entries into the middle of a file, or delete entries from a file. Utilities to free space for deleted files, if the file system provides an undelete function, also belong to this category.
</p><p>Some file systems defer operations such as reorganization of free space, secure erasing of free space, and rebuilding of hierarchical structures by providing utilities to perform these functions at times of minimal activity. An example is the file system <a href="/wiki/Defragmentation" title="Defragmentation">defragmentation</a> utilities.
</p><p>Some of the most important features of file system utilities are supervisory activities which may involve bypassing ownership or direct access to the underlying device. These include high-performance backup and recovery, data replication, and reorganization of various data structures and allocation tables within the file system.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="File_system_API">File system API</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: File system API"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Utilities, libraries and programs use <a href="/wiki/File_system_API" title="File system API">file system APIs</a> to make requests of the file system. These include data transfer, positioning, updating metadata, managing directories, managing access specifications, and removal.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Multiple_file_systems_within_a_single_system">Multiple file systems within a single system</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Multiple file systems within a single system"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Frequently, retail systems are configured with a single file system occupying the entire <a href="/wiki/Computer_storage_device" class="mw-redirect" title="Computer storage device">storage device</a>.
</p><p>Another approach is to <a href="/wiki/Disk_partitioning" title="Disk partitioning">partition</a> the disk so that several file systems with different attributes can be used. One file system, for use as browser cache or email storage, might be configured with a small allocation size. This keeps the activity of creating and deleting files typical of browser activity in a narrow area of the disk where it will not interfere with other file allocations. Another partition might be created for the storage of audio or video files with a relatively large block size. Yet another may normally be set <i>read-only</i> and only periodically be set writable. Some file systems, such as <a href="/wiki/ZFS" title="ZFS">ZFS</a> and <a href="/wiki/APFS" class="mw-redirect" title="APFS">APFS</a>, support multiple file systems sharing a common pool of free blocks, supporting several file systems with different attributes without having to reserved a fixed amount of space for each file system.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A third approach, which is mostly used in cloud systems, is to use "<a href="/wiki/Disk_image" title="Disk image">disk images</a>" to house additional file systems, with the same attributes or not, within another (host) file system as a file. A common example is virtualization: one user can run an experimental Linux distribution (using the <a href="/wiki/Ext4" title="Ext4">ext4</a> file system) in a virtual machine under his/her production Windows environment (using <a href="/wiki/NTFS" title="NTFS">NTFS</a>). The ext4 file system resides in a disk image, which is treated as a file (or multiple files, depending on the <a href="/wiki/Hypervisor" title="Hypervisor">hypervisor</a> and settings) in the NTFS host file system.
</p><p>Having multiple file systems on a single system has the additional benefit that in the event of a corruption of a single file system, the remaining file systems will frequently still be intact. This includes virus destruction of the <i>system</i> file system or even a system that will not boot. File system utilities which require dedicated access can be effectively completed piecemeal. In addition, <a href="/wiki/Defragmentation" title="Defragmentation">defragmentation</a> may be more effective. Several system maintenance utilities, such as virus scans and backups, can also be processed in segments. For example, it is not necessary to backup the file system containing videos along with all the other files if none have been added since the last backup. As for the image files, one can easily "spin off" differential images which contain only "new" data written to the master (original) image. Differential images can be used for both safety concerns (as a "disposable" system - can be quickly restored if destroyed or contaminated by a virus, as the old image can be removed and a new image can be created in matter of seconds, even without automated procedures) and quick virtual machine deployment (since the differential images can be quickly spawned using a script in batches).
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Types">Types</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Types"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Disk_file_systems">Disk file systems</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Disk file systems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A <i>disk file system</i> takes advantages of the ability of disk storage media to randomly address data in a short amount of time. Additional considerations include the speed of accessing data following that initially requested and the anticipation that the following data may also be requested. This permits multiple users (or processes) access to various data on the disk without regard to the sequential location of the data. Examples include <a href="/wiki/File_Allocation_Table" title="File Allocation Table">FAT</a> (<a href="/wiki/FAT12" class="mw-redirect" title="FAT12">FAT12</a>, <a href="/wiki/FAT16" class="mw-redirect" title="FAT16">FAT16</a>, <a href="/wiki/FAT32" class="mw-redirect" title="FAT32">FAT32</a>), <a href="/wiki/ExFAT" title="ExFAT">exFAT</a>, <a href="/wiki/NTFS" title="NTFS">NTFS</a>, <a href="/wiki/ReFS" title="ReFS">ReFS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hierarchical_File_System_(Apple)" title="Hierarchical File System (Apple)">HFS</a> and <a href="/wiki/HFS_Plus" title="HFS Plus">HFS+</a>, <a href="/wiki/High_Performance_File_System" title="High Performance File System">HPFS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apple_File_System" title="Apple File System">APFS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unix_File_System" title="Unix File System">UFS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ext2" title="Ext2">ext2</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ext3" title="Ext3">ext3</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ext4" title="Ext4">ext4</a>, <a href="/wiki/XFS" title="XFS">XFS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Btrfs" title="Btrfs">btrfs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Files-11" title="Files-11">Files-11</a>, <a href="/wiki/Veritas_File_System" title="Veritas File System">Veritas File System</a>, <a href="/wiki/VMFS" class="mw-redirect" title="VMFS">VMFS</a>, <a href="/wiki/ZFS" title="ZFS">ZFS</a>, <a href="/wiki/ReiserFS" title="ReiserFS">ReiserFS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Novell_Storage_Services" title="Novell Storage Services">NSS</a> and ScoutFS. Some disk file systems are <a href="/wiki/Journaling_file_system" title="Journaling file system">journaling file systems</a> or <a href="/wiki/Versioning_file_system" title="Versioning file system">versioning file systems</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Optical_discs">Optical discs</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Optical discs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/ISO_9660" title="ISO 9660">ISO 9660</a> and <a href="/wiki/Universal_Disk_Format" title="Universal Disk Format">Universal Disk Format</a> (UDF) are two common formats that target <a href="/wiki/Compact_Disc" class="mw-redirect" title="Compact Disc">Compact Discs</a>, <a href="/wiki/DVD" title="DVD">DVDs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blu-ray" title="Blu-ray">Blu-ray</a> <a href="/wiki/Optical_disc" title="Optical disc">discs</a>. <a href="/wiki/Mount_Rainier_(packet_writing)" title="Mount Rainier (packet writing)">Mount Rainier</a> is an extension to UDF supported since 2.6 series of the Linux kernel and since Windows Vista that facilitates rewriting to DVDs.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Flash_file_systems">Flash file systems</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Flash file systems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Flash_file_system" title="Flash file system">Flash file system</a></div>
<p>A <i>flash file system</i> considers the special abilities, performance and restrictions of <a href="/wiki/Flash_memory" title="Flash memory">flash memory</a> devices. Frequently a disk file system can use a flash memory device as the underlying storage media, but it is much better to use a file system specifically designed for a flash device.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Tape_file_systems">Tape file systems</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Tape file systems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A <i>tape file system</i> is a file system and tape format designed to store files on tape. <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_tape" title="Magnetic tape">Magnetic tapes</a> are sequential storage media with significantly longer random data access times than disks, posing challenges to the creation and efficient management of a general-purpose file system.
</p><p>In a disk file system there is typically a master file directory, and a map of used and free data regions. Any file additions, changes, or removals require updating the directory and the used/free maps. Random access to data regions is measured in milliseconds so this system works well for disks.
</p><p>Tape requires linear motion to wind and unwind potentially very long reels of media. This tape motion may take several seconds to several minutes to move the read/write head from one end of the tape to the other.
</p><p>Consequently, a master file directory and usage map can be extremely slow and inefficient with tape. Writing typically involves reading the block usage map to find free blocks for writing, updating the usage map and directory to add the data, and then advancing the tape to write the data in the correct spot. Each additional file write requires updating the map and directory and writing the data, which may take several seconds to occur for each file.
</p><p>Tape file systems instead typically allow for the file directory to be spread across the tape intermixed with the data, referred to as <i>streaming</i>, so that time-consuming and repeated tape motions are not required to write new data.
</p><p>However, a side effect of this design is that reading the file directory of a tape usually requires scanning the entire tape to read all the scattered directory entries. Most data archiving software that works with tape storage will store a local copy of the tape catalog on a disk file system, so that adding files to a tape can be done quickly without having to rescan the tape media. The local tape catalog copy is usually discarded if not used for a specified period of time, at which point the tape must be re-scanned if it is to be used in the future.
</p><p>IBM has developed a file system for tape called the <a href="/wiki/Linear_Tape_File_System" title="Linear Tape File System">Linear Tape File System</a>. The IBM implementation of this file system has been released as the open-source <a href="/wiki/Linear_Tape_File_System#IBM_Linear_Tape_File_System_-_Single_Drive_Edition" title="Linear Tape File System">IBM Linear Tape File System — Single Drive Edition (LTFS-SDE)</a> product. The Linear Tape File System uses a separate partition on the tape to record the index meta-data, thereby avoiding the problems associated with scattering directory entries across the entire tape.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Tape_formatting">Tape formatting</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Tape formatting"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Writing data to a tape, erasing, or formatting a tape is often a significantly time-consuming process and can take several hours on large tapes.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>a<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> With many data tape technologies it is not necessary to format the tape before over-writing new data to the tape. This is due to the inherently destructive nature of overwriting data on sequential media.
</p><p>Because of the time it can take to format a tape, typically tapes are pre-formatted so that the tape user does not need to spend time preparing each new tape for use. All that is usually necessary is to write an identifying media label to the tape before use, and even this can be automatically written by software when a new tape is used for the first time.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Database_file_systems">Database file systems</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Database file systems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Another concept for file management is the idea of a database-based file system. Instead of, or in addition to, hierarchical structured management, files are identified by their characteristics, like type of file, topic, author, or similar <a href="/wiki/Metadata_(computing)" class="mw-redirect" title="Metadata (computing)">rich metadata</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>IBM DB2 for i <sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> (formerly known as DB2/400 and DB2 for i5/OS) is a database file system as part of the object based <a href="/wiki/IBM_i" title="IBM i">IBM i</a><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> operating system (formerly known as OS/400 and i5/OS), incorporating a <a href="/wiki/Single-level_store" title="Single-level store">single level store</a> and running on IBM Power Systems (formerly known as AS/400 and iSeries), designed by Frank G. Soltis IBM's former chief scientist for IBM i. Around 1978 to 1988 Frank G. Soltis and his team at IBM Rochester had successfully designed and applied technologies like the database file system where others like Microsoft later failed to accomplish.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These technologies are informally known as 'Fortress Rochester'<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Any news report or blog article mentioning this name and relating it to IBM i? (June 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and were in few basic aspects extended from early Mainframe technologies but in many ways more advanced from a technological perspective<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Is there any articles supporting this technological superiority? (June 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>.
</p><p>Some other projects that are not "pure" database file systems but that use some aspects of a database file system:
</p>
<ul><li>Many <a href="/wiki/Web_content_management_system" title="Web content management system">Web content management systems</a> use a <a href="/wiki/Database_management_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Database management system">relational DBMS</a> to store and retrieve files. For example, <a href="/wiki/XHTML" title="XHTML">XHTML</a> files are stored as <a href="/wiki/XML" title="XML">XML</a> or text fields, while image files are stored as blob fields; <a href="/wiki/SQL" title="SQL">SQL</a> SELECT (with optional <a href="/wiki/XPath" title="XPath">XPath</a>) statements retrieve the files, and allow the use of a sophisticated logic and more rich information associations than "usual file systems." Many CMSs also have the option of storing only <a href="/wiki/Metadata" title="Metadata">metadata</a> within the database, with the standard filesystem used to store the content of files.</li>
<li>Very large file systems, embodied by applications like <a href="/wiki/Apache_Hadoop" title="Apache Hadoop">Apache Hadoop</a> and <a href="/wiki/Google_File_System" title="Google File System">Google File System</a>, use some <i>database file system</i> concepts.</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Transactional_file_systems">Transactional file systems</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Transactional file systems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Some programs need to either make multiple file system changes, or, if one or more of the changes fail for any reason, make none of the changes. For example, a program which is installing or updating software may write executables, libraries, and/or configuration files. If some of the writing fails and the software is left partially installed or updated, the software may be broken or unusable. An incomplete update of a key system utility, such as the command <a href="/wiki/Shell_(computing)" title="Shell (computing)">shell</a>, may leave the entire system in an unusable state.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Transaction_processing" title="Transaction processing">Transaction processing</a> introduces the <a href="/wiki/Atomicity_(programming)" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomicity (programming)">atomicity</a> guarantee, ensuring that operations inside of a transaction are either all committed or the transaction can be aborted and the system discards all of its partial results. This means that if there is a crash or power failure, after recovery, the stored state will be consistent. Either the software will be completely installed or the failed installation will be completely rolled back, but an unusable partial install will not be left on the system. Transactions also provide the <a href="/wiki/Isolation_(database_systems)" title="Isolation (database systems)">isolation</a> guarantee<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="Complicated terms (June 2017)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>, meaning that operations within a transaction are hidden from other threads on the system until the transaction commits, and that interfering operations on the system will be properly <a href="/wiki/Serialization" title="Serialization">serialized</a> with the transaction.
</p><p>Windows, beginning with Vista, added transaction support to <a href="/wiki/NTFS" title="NTFS">NTFS</a>, in a feature called <a href="/wiki/Transactional_NTFS" title="Transactional NTFS">Transactional NTFS</a>, but its use is now discouraged.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There are a number of research prototypes of transactional file systems for UNIX systems, including the Valor file system,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Amino,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> LFS,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and a transactional <a href="/wiki/Ext3" title="Ext3">ext3</a> file system on the TxOS kernel,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> as well as transactional file systems targeting embedded systems, such as TFFS.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Ensuring consistency across multiple file system operations is difficult, if not impossible, without file system transactions. <a href="/wiki/File_locking" title="File locking">File locking</a> can be used as a <a href="/wiki/Concurrency_control" title="Concurrency control">concurrency control</a> mechanism for individual files, but it typically does not protect the directory structure or file metadata. For instance, file locking cannot prevent <a href="/wiki/TOCTTOU" class="mw-redirect" title="TOCTTOU">TOCTTOU</a> race conditions on symbolic links.
File locking also cannot automatically roll back a failed operation, such as a software upgrade; this requires atomicity.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Journaling_file_system" title="Journaling file system">Journaling file systems</a> is one technique used to introduce transaction-level consistency to file system structures. Journal transactions are not exposed to programs as part of the OS API; they are only used internally to ensure consistency at the granularity of a single system call.
</p><p>Data backup systems typically do not provide support for direct backup of data stored in a transactional manner, which makes the recovery of reliable and consistent data sets difficult. Most backup software simply notes what files have changed since a certain time, regardless of the transactional state shared across multiple files in the overall dataset. As a workaround, some database systems simply produce an archived state file containing all data up to that point, and the backup software only backs that up and does not interact directly with the active transactional databases at all. Recovery requires separate recreation of the database from the state file after the file has been restored by the backup software.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Network_file_systems">Network file systems</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Network file systems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Distributed_file_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Distributed file system">Distributed file system</a></div>
<p>A <i>network file system</i> is a file system that acts as a client for a remote file access protocol, providing access to files on a server. Programs using local interfaces can transparently create, manage and access hierarchical directories and files in remote network-connected computers. Examples of network file systems include clients for the <a href="/wiki/Network_File_System_(protocol)" class="mw-redirect" title="Network File System (protocol)">NFS</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Andrew_File_System" title="Andrew File System">AFS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Server_Message_Block" title="Server Message Block">SMB</a> protocols, and file-system-like clients for <a href="/wiki/File_Transfer_Protocol" title="File Transfer Protocol">FTP</a> and <a href="/wiki/WebDAV" title="WebDAV">WebDAV</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Shared_disk_file_systems">Shared disk file systems</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Shared disk file systems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shared_disk_file_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Shared disk file system">Shared disk file system</a></div>
<p>A <i>shared disk file system</i> is one in which a number of machines (usually servers) all have access to the same external disk subsystem (usually a <a href="/wiki/Storage_area_network" title="Storage area network">storage area network</a>). The file system arbitrates access to that subsystem, preventing write collisions.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Examples include <a href="/wiki/GFS2" title="GFS2">GFS2</a> from <a href="/wiki/Red_Hat" title="Red Hat">Red Hat</a>, <a href="/wiki/IBM_General_Parallel_File_System" class="mw-redirect" title="IBM General Parallel File System">GPFS</a>, now known as Spectrum Scale, from IBM, <a href="/wiki/SAN_File_System" class="mw-redirect" title="SAN File System">SFS</a> from DataPlow, <a href="/wiki/CXFS" title="CXFS">CXFS</a> from <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Graphics_International" title="Silicon Graphics International">SGI</a>, <a href="/wiki/StorNext" class="mw-redirect" title="StorNext">StorNext</a> from <a href="/wiki/Quantum_Corporation" title="Quantum Corporation">Quantum Corporation</a> and ScoutFS from Versity.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Special_file_systems">Special file systems</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Special file systems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><span class="anchor" id="special_file_system"></span>
</p><p>Some file systems expose elements of the operating system as files so they can be acted on via the <a href="/wiki/File_system_API" title="File system API">file system API</a>. This is common in <a href="/wiki/Unix-like" title="Unix-like">Unix-like</a> operating systems, and to a lesser extent in other operating systems. Examples include:
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="device_file_system"></span>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Devfs" class="mw-redirect" title="Devfs">devfs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Udev" title="Udev">udev</a>, <a href="/wiki/TOPS-10" title="TOPS-10">TOPS-10</a> expose I/O devices or pseudo-devices as special files</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Configfs" title="Configfs">configfs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sysfs" title="Sysfs">sysfs</a> expose special files that can be used to query and configure <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">Linux</a> kernel information</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Procfs" title="Procfs">procfs</a> exposes process information as special files</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Minimal_file_system_/_audio-cassette_storage"><span id="Minimal_file_system_.2F_audio-cassette_storage"></span>Minimal file system / audio-cassette storage</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Minimal file system / audio-cassette storage"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In the 1970s disk and digital tape devices were too expensive for some early <a href="/wiki/Microcomputer" title="Microcomputer">microcomputer</a> users. An inexpensive basic data storage system was devised that used common <a href="/wiki/Audio_cassette" class="mw-redirect" title="Audio cassette">audio cassette</a> tape.
</p><p>When the system needed to write data, the user was notified to press "RECORD" on the cassette recorder, then press "RETURN" on the keyboard to notify the system that the cassette recorder was recording. The system wrote a sound to provide time synchronization, then <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City_standard" title="Kansas City standard">modulated sounds</a> that encoded a prefix, the data, a <a href="/wiki/Checksum" title="Checksum">checksum</a> and a suffix. When the system needed to read data, the user was instructed to press "PLAY" on the cassette recorder. The system would <i>listen</i> to the sounds on the tape waiting until a burst of sound could be recognized as the synchronization. The system would then interpret subsequent sounds as data. When the data read was complete, the system would notify the user to press "STOP" on the cassette recorder. It was primitive, but it (mostly) worked. Data was stored sequentially, usually in an unnamed format, although some systems (such as the <a href="/wiki/Commodore_PET" title="Commodore PET">Commodore PET</a> series of computers) did allow the files to be named. Multiple sets of data could be written and located by fast-forwarding the tape and observing at the tape counter to find the approximate start of the next data region on the tape. The user might have to listen to the sounds to find the right spot to begin playing the next data region. Some implementations even included audible sounds interspersed with the data.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Flat_file_systems">Flat file systems</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Flat file systems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Flat_file_database" class="mw-redirect" title="Flat file database">Flat file database</a>.</div>
<p>In a flat file system, there are no <a href="/wiki/Subdirectory" class="mw-redirect" title="Subdirectory">subdirectories</a>; directory entries for all files are stored in a single directory.
</p><p>When <a href="/wiki/Floppy_disk" title="Floppy disk">floppy disk</a> media was first available this type of file system was adequate due to the relatively small amount of data space available. <a href="/wiki/CP/M" title="CP/M">CP/M</a> machines featured a flat file system, where files could be assigned to one of 16 <i>user areas</i> and generic file operations narrowed to work on one instead of defaulting to work on all of them. These user areas were no more than special attributes associated with the files; that is, it was not necessary to define specific <a href="/wiki/Disk_quota" title="Disk quota">quota</a> for each of these areas and files could be added to groups for as long as there was still free storage space on the disk. The early <a href="/wiki/Apple_Macintosh" class="mw-redirect" title="Apple Macintosh">Apple Macintosh</a> also featured a flat file system, the <a href="/wiki/Macintosh_File_System" title="Macintosh File System">Macintosh File System</a>. It was unusual in that the file management program (<a href="/wiki/Macintosh_Finder" class="mw-redirect" title="Macintosh Finder">Macintosh Finder</a>) created the illusion of a partially hierarchical filing system on top of EMFS. This structure required every file to have a unique name, even if it appeared to be in a separate folder. <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> <a href="/wiki/DOS/360" class="mw-redirect" title="DOS/360">DOS/360</a> and <a href="/wiki/OS/360" class="mw-redirect" title="OS/360">OS/360</a> store entries for all files on a disk pack (<i>volume</i>) in a directory on the pack called a <i><a href="/wiki/Volume_Table_of_Contents" title="Volume Table of Contents">Volume Table of Contents</a></i> (VTOC).
</p><p>While simple, flat file systems become awkward as the number of files grows and makes it difficult to organize data into related groups of files.
</p><p>A recent addition to the flat file system family is <a href="/wiki/Amazon.com" class="mw-redirect" title="Amazon.com">Amazon</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Amazon_S3" title="Amazon S3">S3</a>, a remote storage service, which is intentionally simplistic to allow users the ability to customize how their data is stored. The only constructs are buckets (imagine a disk drive of unlimited size) and objects (similar, but not identical to the standard concept of a file). Advanced file management is allowed by being able to use nearly any character (including '/') in the object's name, and the ability to select subsets of the bucket's content based on identical prefixes.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Implementations">Implementations</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Implementations"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating system</a> (OS) typically supports one or more file systems. Sometimes an OS and its file system are so tightly interwoven that it is difficult to describe them independently.
</p><p>An OS typically provides file system access to the user. Often an OS provides <a href="/wiki/Command_line_interface" class="mw-redirect" title="Command line interface">command line interface</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Unix_shell" title="Unix shell">Unix shell</a>, Windows <a href="/wiki/Command_Prompt" class="mw-redirect" title="Command Prompt">Command Prompt</a> and <a href="/wiki/PowerShell" title="PowerShell">PowerShell</a>, and <a href="/wiki/DIGITAL_Command_Language" title="DIGITAL Command Language">OpenVMS DCL</a>. An OS often also provides <a href="/wiki/Graphical_user_interface" title="Graphical user interface">graphical user interface</a> <a href="/wiki/File_browser" class="mw-redirect" title="File browser">file browsers</a> such as MacOS <a href="/wiki/Finder_(software)" title="Finder (software)">Finder</a> and Windows <a href="/wiki/File_Explorer" title="File Explorer">File Explorer</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Unix_and_Unix-like_operating_systems">Unix and Unix-like operating systems</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Unix and Unix-like operating systems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Unix-like" title="Unix-like">Unix-like</a> operating systems create a virtual file system, which makes all the files on all the devices appear to exist in a single hierarchy. This means, in those systems, there is one <a href="/wiki/Root_directory" title="Root directory">root directory</a>, and every file existing on the system is located under it somewhere. Unix-like systems can use a <a href="/wiki/RAM_disk" class="mw-redirect" title="RAM disk">RAM disk</a> or network shared resource as its root directory.
</p><p>Unix-like systems assign a device name to each device, but this is not how the files on that device are accessed. Instead, to gain access to files on another device, the operating system must first be informed where in the directory tree those files should appear. This process is called <a href="/wiki/Mount_(computing)" title="Mount (computing)">mounting</a> a file system. For example, to access the files on a <a href="/wiki/CD-ROM" title="CD-ROM">CD-ROM</a>, one must tell the operating system "Take the file system from this CD-ROM and make it appear under such-and-such directory." The directory given to the operating system is called the <i><a href="/wiki/Mount_point" class="mw-redirect" title="Mount point">mount point</a></i>&#160;– it might, for example, be <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886049734">.mw-parser-output .monospaced{font-family:monospace,monospace}</style><span class="monospaced">/media</span>. The <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced">/media</span> directory exists on many Unix systems (as specified in the <a href="/wiki/Filesystem_Hierarchy_Standard" title="Filesystem Hierarchy Standard">Filesystem Hierarchy Standard</a>) and is intended specifically for use as a mount point for removable media such as CDs, DVDs, USB drives or floppy disks. It may be empty, or it may contain subdirectories for mounting individual devices. Generally, only the <a href="/wiki/System_administrator" title="System administrator">administrator</a> (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Root_user" class="mw-redirect" title="Root user">root user</a>) may authorize the mounting of file systems.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Unix-like" title="Unix-like">Unix-like</a> operating systems often include software and tools that assist in the mounting process and provide it new functionality. Some of these strategies have been coined "auto-mounting" as a reflection of their purpose.
</p>
<ul><li>In many situations, file systems other than the root need to be available as soon as the operating system has <a href="/wiki/Booting" title="Booting">booted</a>. All Unix-like systems therefore provide a facility for mounting file systems at boot time. <a href="/wiki/System_administrator" title="System administrator">System administrators</a> define these file systems in the configuration file <a href="/wiki/Fstab" title="Fstab">fstab</a> (<i>vfstab</i> in <a href="/wiki/Solaris_(operating_system)" class="mw-redirect" title="Solaris (operating system)">Solaris</a>), which also indicates options and mount points.</li>
<li>In some situations, there is no need to mount certain file systems at <a href="/wiki/Booting" title="Booting">boot time</a>, although their use may be desired thereafter. There are some utilities for Unix-like systems that allow the mounting of predefined file systems upon demand.</li>
<li>Removable media allow programs and data to be transferred between machines without a physical connection. Common examples include <a href="/wiki/USB_flash_drive" title="USB flash drive">USB flash drives</a>, <a href="/wiki/CD-ROM" title="CD-ROM">CD-ROMs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/DVD" title="DVD">DVDs</a>. Utilities have therefore been developed to detect the presence and availability of a medium and then mount that medium without any user intervention.</li>
<li>Progressive Unix-like systems have also introduced a concept called <b>supermounting</b>; see, for example, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://sourceforge.net/projects/supermount-ng">the Linux supermount-ng project</a>. For example, a floppy disk that has been supermounted can be physically removed from the system. Under normal circumstances, the disk should have been synchronized and then unmounted before its removal. Provided synchronization has occurred, a different disk can be inserted into the drive. The system automatically notices that the disk has changed and updates the mount point contents to reflect the new medium.</li>
<li>An <a href="/wiki/Automounter" title="Automounter">automounter</a> will automatically mount a file system when a reference is made to the directory atop which it should be mounted. This is usually used for file systems on network servers, rather than relying on events such as the insertion of media, as would be appropriate for removable media.</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Linux"><span class="anchor" id="LINUX"></span>Linux</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Linux"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">Linux</a> supports numerous file systems, but common choices for the system disk on a block device include the ext* family (<a href="/wiki/Ext2" title="Ext2">ext2</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ext3" title="Ext3">ext3</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ext4" title="Ext4">ext4</a>), <a href="/wiki/XFS" title="XFS">XFS</a>, <a href="/wiki/JFS_(file_system)" title="JFS (file system)">JFS</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Btrfs" title="Btrfs">btrfs</a>. For raw flash without a <a href="/wiki/Flash_translation_layer" class="mw-redirect" title="Flash translation layer">flash translation layer</a> (FTL) or <a href="/wiki/Memory_Technology_Device" title="Memory Technology Device">Memory Technology Device</a> (MTD), there are <a href="/wiki/UBIFS" title="UBIFS">UBIFS</a>, <a href="/wiki/JFFS2" title="JFFS2">JFFS2</a> and <a href="/wiki/YAFFS" title="YAFFS">YAFFS</a>, among others. <a href="/wiki/SquashFS" title="SquashFS">SquashFS</a> is a common compressed read-only file system.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Solaris">Solaris</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Solaris"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Solaris_(operating_system)" class="mw-redirect" title="Solaris (operating system)">Solaris</a> in earlier releases defaulted to (non-journaled or non-logging) <a href="/wiki/Unix_File_System" title="Unix File System">UFS</a> for bootable and supplementary file systems. Solaris defaulted to, supported, and extended UFS.
</p><p>Support for other file systems and significant enhancements were added over time, including <a href="/wiki/Veritas_Software" class="mw-redirect" title="Veritas Software">Veritas Software</a> Corp. (journaling) <a href="/wiki/VxFS" class="mw-redirect" title="VxFS">VxFS</a>, Sun Microsystems (clustering) <a href="/wiki/QFS" title="QFS">QFS</a>, Sun Microsystems (journaling) UFS, and Sun Microsystems (open source, poolable, 128 bit compressible, and error-correcting) <a href="/wiki/ZFS" title="ZFS">ZFS</a>.
</p><p>Kernel extensions were added to Solaris to allow for bootable Veritas <a href="/wiki/VxFS" class="mw-redirect" title="VxFS">VxFS</a> operation. Logging or <a href="/wiki/Journaling_file_system" title="Journaling file system">journaling</a> was added to UFS in Sun's <a href="/wiki/Solaris_7" class="mw-redirect" title="Solaris 7">Solaris 7</a>. Releases of <a href="/wiki/Solaris_10" class="mw-redirect" title="Solaris 10">Solaris 10</a>, Solaris Express, <a href="/wiki/OpenSolaris" title="OpenSolaris">OpenSolaris</a>, and other open source variants of the Solaris operating system later supported bootable <a href="/wiki/ZFS" title="ZFS">ZFS</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Logical_Volume_Management" class="mw-redirect" title="Logical Volume Management">Logical Volume Management</a> allows for spanning a file system across multiple devices for the purpose of adding redundancy, capacity, and/or throughput. Legacy environments in Solaris may use <a href="/wiki/Solaris_Volume_Manager" title="Solaris Volume Manager">Solaris Volume Manager</a> (formerly known as <a href="/wiki/Solstice_DiskSuite" class="mw-redirect" title="Solstice DiskSuite">Solstice DiskSuite</a>). Multiple operating systems (including Solaris) may use <a href="/wiki/Veritas_Volume_Manager" title="Veritas Volume Manager">Veritas Volume Manager</a>. Modern Solaris based operating systems eclipse the need for volume management through leveraging virtual storage pools in <a href="/wiki/ZFS" title="ZFS">ZFS</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="macOS">macOS</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: macOS"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
