<p><a href="/wiki/MacOS" title="MacOS">macOS (formerly Mac OS X)</a> uses the <a href="/wiki/Apple_File_System" title="Apple File System">Apple File System</a> (APFS), which in 2017 replaced a file system inherited from <a href="/wiki/Classic_Mac_OS" title="Classic Mac OS">classic Mac OS</a> called <a href="/wiki/HFS_Plus" title="HFS Plus">HFS Plus</a> (HFS+). Apple also uses the term "Mac OS Extended" for HFS+.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> HFS Plus is a <a href="/wiki/Metadata_(computing)" class="mw-redirect" title="Metadata (computing)">metadata</a>-rich and <a href="/wiki/Case_preservation" title="Case preservation">case-preserving</a> but (usually) <a href="/wiki/Case_sensitivity" title="Case sensitivity">case-insensitive</a> file system. Due to the Unix roots of macOS, Unix permissions were added to HFS Plus. Later versions of HFS Plus added journaling to prevent corruption of the file system structure and introduced a number of optimizations to the allocation algorithms in an attempt to defragment files automatically without requiring an external defragmenter.
</p><p>File names can be up to 255 characters. HFS Plus uses <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> to store file names. On macOS, the <a href="/wiki/File_format" title="File format">filetype</a> can come from the <a href="/wiki/Type_code" class="mw-redirect" title="Type code">type code</a>, stored in file's metadata, or the <a href="/wiki/Filename_extension" title="Filename extension">filename extension</a>.
</p><p>HFS Plus has three kinds of links: Unix-style <a href="/wiki/Hard_link" title="Hard link">hard links</a>, Unix-style <a href="/wiki/Symbolic_link" title="Symbolic link">symbolic links</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alias_(Mac_OS)" title="Alias (Mac OS)">aliases</a>. Aliases are designed to maintain a link to their original file even if they are moved or renamed; they are not interpreted by the file system itself, but by the File Manager code in <a href="/wiki/Userland_(computing)" class="mw-redirect" title="Userland (computing)">userland</a>.
</p><p>macOS 10.13 High Sierra, which was announced on June 5, 2017, at Apple's WWDC event, uses the <a href="/wiki/Apple_File_System" title="Apple File System">Apple File System</a> on <a href="/wiki/Solid-state_drive" title="Solid-state drive">solid-state drives</a>.
</p><p>macOS also supported the <a href="/wiki/Unix_File_System" title="Unix File System">UFS</a> file system, derived from the <a href="/wiki/BSD" class="mw-redirect" title="BSD">BSD</a> Unix Fast File System via <a href="/wiki/NeXTSTEP" title="NeXTSTEP">NeXTSTEP</a>. However, as of <a href="/wiki/Mac_OS_X_Leopard" title="Mac OS X Leopard">Mac OS X Leopard</a>, macOS could no longer be installed on a UFS volume, nor can a pre-Leopard system installed on a UFS volume be upgraded to Leopard.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As of <a href="/wiki/Mac_OS_X_Lion" class="mw-redirect" title="Mac OS X Lion">Mac OS X Lion</a> UFS support was completely dropped.
</p><p>Newer versions of macOS are capable of reading and writing to the legacy <a href="/wiki/File_Allocation_Table" title="File Allocation Table">FAT</a> file systems (16 and 32) common on Windows. They are also capable of <i>reading</i> the newer <a href="/wiki/NTFS" title="NTFS">NTFS</a> file systems for Windows. In order to <i>write</i> to NTFS file systems on macOS versions prior to <a href="/wiki/Mac_OS_X_Snow_Leopard" title="Mac OS X Snow Leopard">Mac OS X Snow Leopard</a> third-party software is necessary. Mac OS X 10.6 (Snow Leopard) and later allow writing to NTFS file systems, but only after a non-trivial system setting change (third-party software exists that automates this).<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Finally, macOS supports reading and writing of the <a href="/wiki/ExFAT" title="ExFAT">exFAT</a> file system since Mac OS X Snow Leopard, starting from version 10.6.5.<sup id="cite_ref-encase-book_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-encase-book-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="OS/2"><span id="OS.2F2"></span>OS/2</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: OS/2"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/OS/2" title="OS/2">OS/2</a> 1.2 introduced the <a href="/wiki/High_Performance_File_System" title="High Performance File System">High Performance File System</a> (HPFS). HPFS supports mixed case file names in different <a href="/wiki/Code_page" title="Code page">code pages</a>, long file names (255 characters), more efficient use of disk space, an architecture that keeps related items close to each other on the disk volume, less fragmentation of data, <a href="/wiki/Extent_(file_systems)" title="Extent (file systems)">extent-based</a> space allocation, a <a href="/wiki/B%2B_tree" title="B+ tree">B+ tree</a> structure for directories, and the root directory located at the midpoint of the disk, for faster average access. A <a href="/wiki/JFS_(file_system)" title="JFS (file system)">journaled filesystem</a> (JFS) was shipped in 1999.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="PC-BSD">PC-BSD</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: PC-BSD"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/PC-BSD" class="mw-redirect" title="PC-BSD">PC-BSD</a> is a desktop version of FreeBSD, which inherits <a href="/wiki/FreeBSD" title="FreeBSD">FreeBSD</a>'s <a href="/wiki/ZFS" title="ZFS">ZFS</a> support, similarly to <a href="/wiki/FreeNAS" class="mw-redirect" title="FreeNAS">FreeNAS</a>. The new graphical installer of <a href="/wiki/PC-BSD" class="mw-redirect" title="PC-BSD">PC-BSD</a> can handle <i>/ (<a href="/wiki/Root_directory" title="Root directory">root</a>) on ZFS</i> and <a href="/wiki/RAID-Z" class="mw-redirect" title="RAID-Z">RAID-Z</a> pool installs and <a href="/wiki/Disk_encryption" title="Disk encryption">disk encryption</a> using <a href="/wiki/Geli_(software)" title="Geli (software)">Geli</a> right from the start in an easy convenient (<a href="/wiki/GUI" class="mw-redirect" title="GUI">GUI</a>) way. The current PC-BSD 9.0+ 'Isotope Edition' has ZFS filesystem version 5 and ZFS storage pool version 28.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Plan_9">Plan 9</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Plan 9"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Plan_9_from_Bell_Labs" title="Plan 9 from Bell Labs">Plan&#160;9 from Bell Labs</a> treats everything as a file and accesses all objects as a file would be accessed (i.e., there is no <a href="/wiki/Ioctl" title="Ioctl">ioctl</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mmap" title="Mmap">mmap</a>): networking, graphics, debugging, authentication, capabilities, encryption, and other services are accessed via I/O operations on <a href="/wiki/File_descriptor" title="File descriptor">file descriptors</a>. The <a href="/wiki/9P_(protocol)" title="9P (protocol)">9P</a> protocol removes the difference between local and remote files. File systems in Plan&#160;9 are organized with the help of private, per-process namespaces, allowing each process to have a different view of the many file systems that provide resources in a distributed system.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Inferno_(operating_system)" title="Inferno (operating system)">Inferno</a> operating system shares these concepts with Plan&#160;9.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Microsoft_Windows">Microsoft Windows</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Microsoft Windows"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Dir_command_in_Windows_Command_Prompt.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Dir_command_in_Windows_Command_Prompt.png/300px-Dir_command_in_Windows_Command_Prompt.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="154" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Dir_command_in_Windows_Command_Prompt.png/450px-Dir_command_in_Windows_Command_Prompt.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Dir_command_in_Windows_Command_Prompt.png/600px-Dir_command_in_Windows_Command_Prompt.png 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="370" /></a><figcaption>Directory listing in a <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Windows</a> command shell</figcaption></figure>
<p>Windows makes use of the <a href="/wiki/File_Allocation_Table" title="File Allocation Table">FAT</a>, <a href="/wiki/NTFS" title="NTFS">NTFS</a>, <a href="/wiki/ExFAT" title="ExFAT">exFAT</a>, <a href="/wiki/Live_File_System" title="Live File System">Live File System</a> and <a href="/wiki/ReFS" title="ReFS">ReFS</a> file systems (the last of these is only supported and usable in <a href="/wiki/Windows_Server_2012" title="Windows Server 2012">Windows Server 2012</a>, <a href="/wiki/Windows_Server_2016" title="Windows Server 2016">Windows Server 2016</a>, <a href="/wiki/Windows_8" title="Windows 8">Windows 8</a>, <a href="/wiki/Windows_8.1" title="Windows 8.1">Windows 8.1</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Windows_10" title="Windows 10">Windows 10</a>; Windows cannot boot from it).
</p><p>Windows uses a <i><a href="/wiki/Drive_letter" class="mw-redirect" title="Drive letter">drive letter</a></i> abstraction at the user level to distinguish one disk or partition from another. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Path_(computing)" title="Path (computing)">path</a> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced">C:\WINDOWS</span> represents a directory <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced">WINDOWS</span> on the partition represented by the letter C. Drive C: is most commonly used for the primary <a href="/wiki/Hard_disk_drive" title="Hard disk drive">hard disk drive</a> partition, on which Windows is usually installed and from which it boots. This "tradition" has become so firmly ingrained that bugs exist in many applications which make assumptions that the drive that the operating system is installed on is C. The use of drive letters, and the tradition of using "C" as the drive letter for the primary hard disk drive partition, can be traced to <a href="/wiki/MS-DOS" title="MS-DOS">MS-DOS</a>, where the letters A and B were reserved for up to two floppy disk drives. This in turn derived from <a href="/wiki/CP/M" title="CP/M">CP/M</a> in the 1970s, and ultimately from IBM's <a href="/wiki/CP/CMS" title="CP/CMS">CP/CMS</a> of 1967.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="FAT">FAT</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: FAT"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/File_Allocation_Table" title="File Allocation Table">File Allocation Table</a></div>
<p>The family of <a href="/wiki/File_Allocation_Table" title="File Allocation Table">FAT</a> file systems is supported by almost all operating systems for personal computers, including all versions of <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Windows</a> and <a href="/wiki/MS-DOS" title="MS-DOS">MS-DOS</a>/<a href="/wiki/PC_DOS" class="mw-redirect" title="PC DOS">PC&#160;DOS</a>, <a href="/wiki/OS/2" title="OS/2">OS/2</a>, and <a href="/wiki/DR-DOS" title="DR-DOS">DR-DOS</a>. (PC&#160;DOS is an OEM version of MS-DOS, MS-DOS was originally based on <a href="/wiki/Seattle_Computer_Products" title="Seattle Computer Products">SCP</a>'s <a href="/wiki/86-DOS" title="86-DOS">86-DOS</a>. DR-DOS was based on <a href="/wiki/Digital_Research" title="Digital Research">Digital Research</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_DOS" class="mw-redirect" title="Concurrent DOS">Concurrent DOS</a>, a successor of <a href="/wiki/CP/M-86" title="CP/M-86">CP/M-86</a>.) The FAT file systems are therefore well-suited as a universal exchange format between computers and devices of most any type and age.
</p><p>The FAT file system traces its roots back to an (incompatible) 8-bit FAT precursor in <a href="/wiki/Standalone_Disk_BASIC" class="mw-redirect" title="Standalone Disk BASIC">Standalone Disk BASIC</a> and the short-lived <a href="/wiki/MIDAS_(operating_system)" class="mw-redirect" title="MIDAS (operating system)">MDOS/MIDAS</a> project.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Over the years, the file system has been expanded from <a href="/wiki/FAT12" class="mw-redirect" title="FAT12">FAT12</a> to <a href="/wiki/FAT16" class="mw-redirect" title="FAT16">FAT16</a> and <a href="/wiki/FAT32" class="mw-redirect" title="FAT32">FAT32</a>. Various features have been added to the file system including <a href="/wiki/Subdirectory" class="mw-redirect" title="Subdirectory">subdirectories</a>, <a href="/wiki/Codepage" class="mw-redirect" title="Codepage">codepage</a> support, <a href="/wiki/Extended_attribute" class="mw-redirect" title="Extended attribute">extended attributes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Long_filenames" class="mw-redirect" title="Long filenames">long filenames</a>. Third parties such as Digital Research have incorporated optional support for deletion tracking, and volume/directory/file-based multi-user security schemes to support file and directory passwords and permissions such as read/write/execute/delete access rights. Most of these extensions are not supported by Windows.
</p><p>The FAT12 and FAT16 file systems had a limit on the number of entries in the <a href="/wiki/Root_directory" title="Root directory">root directory</a> of the file system and had restrictions on the maximum size of FAT-formatted disks or <a href="/wiki/Partition_(computing)" class="mw-redirect" title="Partition (computing)">partitions</a>.
</p><p>FAT32 addresses the limitations in FAT12 and FAT16, except for the file size limit of close to 4&#160;GB, but it remains limited compared to NTFS.
</p><p>FAT12, FAT16 and FAT32 also have a limit of eight characters for the file name, and three characters for the extension (such as <a href="/wiki/.exe" title=".exe">.exe</a>). This is commonly referred to as the <a href="/wiki/8.3_filename" title="8.3 filename">8.3 filename</a> limit. <a href="/wiki/VFAT" class="mw-redirect" title="VFAT">VFAT</a>, an optional extension to FAT12, FAT16 and FAT32, introduced in <a href="/wiki/Windows_95" title="Windows 95">Windows 95</a> and <a href="/wiki/Windows_NT_3.5" title="Windows NT 3.5">Windows NT 3.5</a>, allowed long file names (<a href="/wiki/Long_File_Name" class="mw-redirect" title="Long File Name">LFN</a>) to be stored in the FAT file system in a backwards compatible fashion.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="NTFS">NTFS</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: NTFS"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/NTFS" title="NTFS">NTFS</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/NTFS" title="NTFS">NTFS</a>, introduced with the <a href="/wiki/Windows_NT" title="Windows NT">Windows NT</a> operating system in 1993, allowed <a href="/wiki/Access_control_list" class="mw-redirect" title="Access control list">ACL</a>-based permission control. Other features also supported by <a href="/wiki/NTFS" title="NTFS">NTFS</a> include hard links, multiple file streams, attribute indexing, quota tracking, sparse files, encryption, compression, and reparse points (directories working as mount-points for other file systems, symlinks, junctions, remote storage links).
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="exFAT">exFAT</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: exFAT"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/ExFAT" title="ExFAT">exFAT</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/ExFAT" title="ExFAT">exFAT</a> has certain advantages over NTFS with regard to <a href="/wiki/File_system_overhead" class="mw-redirect" title="File system overhead">file system overhead</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>exFAT is not backward compatible with FAT file systems such as FAT12, FAT16 or FAT32. The file system is supported with newer Windows systems, such as Windows XP, Windows Server 2003, Windows Vista, Windows 2008, Windows 7, Windows 8, Windows 8.1, Windows 10 and Windows 11.
</p><p>exFAT is supported in macOS starting with version 10.6.5 (Snow Leopard).<sup id="cite_ref-encase-book_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-encase-book-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Support in other operating systems is sparse since implementing support for exFAT requires a license. exFAT is the only file system that is fully supported on both macOS and Windows that can hold files larger than 4&#160;GB.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="OpenVMS">OpenVMS</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: OpenVMS"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Files-11" title="Files-11">Files-11</a></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="MVS">MVS</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: MVS"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/MVS#MVS_filesystem" title="MVS">MVS §&#160;MVS filesystem</a></div>
<p>Prior to the introduction of <a href="/wiki/VSAM" class="mw-redirect" title="VSAM">VSAM</a>, <a href="/wiki/OS/360" class="mw-redirect" title="OS/360">OS/360</a> systems implemented a hybrid file system. The system was designed to easily support <a href="/wiki/Removable_media" title="Removable media">removable disk packs</a>, so the information relating to all files on one disk (<i>volume</i> in IBM terminology) is stored on that disk in a <a href="/wiki/Flat_file_database" class="mw-redirect" title="Flat file database">flat system file</a> called the <i><a href="/wiki/Volume_Table_of_Contents" title="Volume Table of Contents">Volume Table of Contents</a></i> (VTOC). The VTOC stores all metadata for the file. Later a hierarchical directory structure was imposed with the introduction of the <i>System Catalog</i>, which can optionally catalog files (datasets) on resident and removable volumes. The catalog only contains information to relate a dataset to a specific volume. If the user requests access to a dataset on an offline volume, and they have suitable privileges, the system will attempt to mount the required volume. Cataloged and non-cataloged datasets can still be accessed using information in the VTOC, bypassing the catalog, if the required volume id is provided to the OPEN request. Still later the VTOC was indexed to speed up access.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Conversational_Monitor_System">Conversational Monitor System</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Conversational Monitor System"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/CMS_file_system" title="CMS file system">CMS file system</a></div>
<p>The IBM <a href="/wiki/Conversational_Monitor_System" title="Conversational Monitor System">Conversational Monitor System</a> (CMS) component of <a href="/wiki/VM/370" class="mw-redirect" title="VM/370">VM/370</a> uses a separate flat file system for each <a href="/wiki/Virtual_disk" class="mw-redirect" title="Virtual disk">virtual disk</a> (<i>minidisk</i>). File data and control information are scattered and intermixed. The anchor is a record called the <i>Master File Directory</i> (MFD), always located in the fourth block on the disk. Originally CMS used fixed-length 800-byte blocks, but later versions used larger size blocks up to 4K. Access to a data record requires two levels of <a href="/wiki/Indirection" title="Indirection">indirection</a>, where the file's directory entry (called a <i>File Status Table</i> (FST) entry) points to blocks containing a list of addresses of the individual records.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="AS/400_file_system"><span id="AS.2F400_file_system"></span>AS/400 file system</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: AS/400 file system"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Data on the AS/400 and its successors consists of system objects mapped into the system virtual address space in a <a href="/wiki/Single-level_store" title="Single-level store">single-level store</a>. Many types of <a href="/wiki/Object_(IBM_i)" title="Object (IBM i)">objects</a> are defined including the directories and files found in other file systems. File objects, along with other types of objects, form the basis of the AS/400's support for an integrated <a href="/wiki/Relational_database" title="Relational database">relational database</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Other_file_systems">Other file systems</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Other file systems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<ul><li>The Prospero File System is a file system based on the Virtual System Model.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The system was created by B. Clifford Neuman of the Information Sciences Institute at the University of Southern California.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Flex_machine#RSRE_FLEX_Computer_System" title="Flex machine">RSRE FLEX file system</a> - written in <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_68" title="ALGOL 68">ALGOL 68</a></li>
<li>The file system of the <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Terminal_System" title="Michigan Terminal System">Michigan Terminal System</a> (MTS) is interesting because: (i) it provides "line files" where record lengths and line numbers are associated as metadata with each record in the file, lines can be added, replaced, updated with the same or different length records, and deleted anywhere in the file without the need to read and rewrite the entire file; (ii) using program keys files may be shared or permitted to commands and programs in addition to users and groups; and (iii) there is a comprehensive file locking mechanism that protects both the file's data and its metadata.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Protection1977_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Protection1977-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/TempleOS" title="TempleOS">TempleOS</a> uses RedSea, a file system made by Terry A. Davis.<sup id="cite_ref-00yK1_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-00yK1-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Limitations">Limitations</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Limitations"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Design_limitations">Design limitations</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Design limitations"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>File systems limit <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_file_systems#Limits" title="Comparison of file systems">storable data capacity</a> – generally driven by the typical size of storage devices at the time the file system is designed and anticipated into the foreseeable future.
</p><p>Since storage sizes have increased at near <a href="/wiki/Exponential_growth" title="Exponential growth">exponential</a> rate (see <a href="/wiki/Moore%27s_law" title="Moore&#39;s law">Moore's law</a>), newer storage devices often exceed existing file system limits within only a few years after introduction. This requires new file systems with ever increasing capacity.
</p><p>With higher capacity, the need for capabilities and therefore complexity increases as well. File system complexity typically varies proportionally with available storage capacity. Capacity issues aside, the file systems of early 1980s <a href="/wiki/Home_computer" title="Home computer">home computers</a> with 50&#160;KB to 512&#160;KB of storage would not be a reasonable choice for modern storage systems with hundreds of gigabytes of capacity. Likewise, modern file systems would not be a reasonable choice for these early systems, since the complexity of modern file system structures would quickly consume the limited capacity of early storage systems.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Converting_the_type_of_a_file_system">Converting the type of a file system</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Converting the type of a file system"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>It may be advantageous or necessary to have files in a different file system than they currently exist. Reasons include the need for an increase in the space requirements beyond the limits of the current file system. The depth of path may need to be increased beyond the restrictions of the file system. There may be performance or reliability considerations. Providing access to another operating system which does not support the existing file system is another reason.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="In-place_conversion">In-place conversion</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: In-place conversion"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In some cases conversion can be done in-place, although migrating the file system is more conservative, as it involves a creating a copy of the data and is recommended.<sup id="cite_ref-ms_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ms-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On Windows, FAT and FAT32 file systems can be converted to NTFS via the convert.exe utility, but not the reverse.<sup id="cite_ref-ms_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ms-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On Linux, ext2 can be converted to ext3 (and converted back), and ext3 can be converted to ext4 (but not back),<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and both ext3 and ext4 can be converted to <a href="/wiki/Btrfs" title="Btrfs">btrfs</a>, and converted back until the undo information is deleted.<sup id="cite_ref-btrfs_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-btrfs-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These conversions are possible due to using the same format for the file data itself, and relocating the metadata into empty space, in some cases using <a href="/wiki/Sparse_file" title="Sparse file">sparse file</a> support.<sup id="cite_ref-btrfs_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-btrfs-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Migrating_to_a_different_file_system">Migrating to a different file system</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Migrating to a different file system"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Migration has the disadvantage of requiring additional space although it may be faster. The best case is if there is unused space on media which will contain the final file system.
</p><p>For example, to migrate a FAT32 file system to an ext2 file system, a new ext2 file system is created. Then the data from the FAT32 file system is copied to the ext2 one, and the old file system is deleted.
</p><p>An alternative, when there is not sufficient space to retain the original file system until the new one is created, is to use a work area (such as a removable media). This takes longer but has the benefit of producing a backup.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Long_file_paths_and_long_file_names">Long file paths and long file names</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=File_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Long file paths and long file names"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Hierarchical_file_system" title="Hierarchical file system">hierarchical file systems</a>, files are accessed by means of a <i><a href="/wiki/Path_(computing)" title="Path (computing)">path</a></i> that is a branching list of directories containing the file. Different file systems have different limits on the depth of the path. File systems also have a limit on the length of an individual file name.
</p><p>Copying files with long names or located in paths of significant depth from one file system to another may cause undesirable results. This depends on how the utility doing the copying handles the discrepancy.
</p>
